```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- wakaru: to understand, to comprehend

TONE HINT:
- kusuri: medicine

Input: wakatta   wakatta   jya   sono   kusuri   wo   kudasi   ima   ni   mo   watashi   ni   mo   kiga atta   no   nai   desu   kara

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient.

- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- kitsuen: smoking tobacco
- tabako: tobacco, cigarette

TONE HINT:
- aienka: heavy smoker

Input: kitsuen inshu   tabako   wa   arimasu   ka
Output: /no_think

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- bisaiundou: fine motor skill, fine motor movement

TONE HINT:
- hizagawarau: to have one's knees be about to give way

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- chuushayaku: injectable drug, injectable medicine

- deshouka: don't you think?

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



TONE HINT:
- ninoashi: second step

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- seikou: character and conduct
- seikou: sexual intercourse, sex

TONE HINT:
- shoushou: just a minute

Input: sshoku   seikou   shimmashita   karada   ga   kyou setsu   hannou   cha ubakaku   shohou   sara eda   subete   kusa uru   chisai   no   jikan   ni   fukuyou   shite
Output: /no_think
```

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



TONE HINT:
- kangae: thinking

Input: shindahouga   mashi   da   to   kangae   nannin aka   no   hou de   kangae   suru   koto   wa   arimasu   ka
Output: /no_think
```

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- zensoku: asthma
- mansei: chronic illness
- shikkan: disease, illness
- haien: pneumonia
- genzai: original sin

TONE HINT:
- deshouka: don't you think?

Input: genzai   mata   kako   ni   ikan wo   jou   ni   natta   koto   arimasu   ka   zensoku   mansei   heisoku   hai shikkan   haien

```

TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.

- parasetamooru: paracetamol

- houshoku: eating until one is full

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- hakike: nausea, feeling like throwing up
- ichiruikansenshou: Category I infectious disease

TONE HINT:
- iwakan: uncomfortable feeling

<details>
<summary>Full prompt sent to Ollama (click to expand)</summary>

```
TASK:
Translate the following Japanese (romaji) dialogue into natural English.

You are an interpreter. The speaker may be a Japanese patient describing symptoms or a doctor asking screening questions.

RULES:
- Use FIRST PERSON ("I", "my", "I feel") when the speaker is a patient. 
- Use natural medical questioning ("Have you...", "Do you fee...") when the speaker is a doctor.
- Translate faithfully. Do NOT add information that is not present.
- If input is unnatural or fragmented, reconstruct into natural English meaning using standard Japanese grammar.
- CLINICAL PRIORITY: If a term is ambiguous, prioritize the most clinical or medical interpretation (e.g., "gein" as "cause", not "performance").
- Output ONLY the English sentence — no labels, no explanation, no Japanese.

EXAMPLES:
Input: mune itai
Output: I have chest pain.

Input: arerugi nai
Output: I have no allergies.

Input: zutsu hidoisugite ugokenai
Output: My headache is so bad I can't move.



MEDICAL GLOSSARY HINTS:
- wakaru: to understand, to comprehend

TONE HINT:
- iwai: congratulation

Input: wakatta   sensei   kore   ijou   arukadeia   ranai   to   iin wo   desu   ga   sugi   no   youyaku   itsu   desuka   saisho   chichi   no   hi   de   happyoukai   aru   no   de
Output: /no_think
```

</details>
